1. INTRODUCTION
This report presents the results of a transportation impact analysis (TIA) conducted for the proposed Collection at
Monterey Bay Resort ("Proposed Project") in Sand City, California. The project site is located between State
Route 1 (SR1) and Monterey Bay with direct access from Playa Avenue and Tioga Avenue. The purpose of this
analysis is to identify the potential impacts of the proposed project on the transportation system in the vicinity of
the site and to identify improvements required to mitigate those impacts, if needed. Impacts were evaluated
following the guidelines of the City of Sand City and surrounding local jurisdictions in Monterey County.


PROJECT DESCRIPTION
The proposed project includes a 342-room resort on 26.5 acres. The proposed project is divided into two phases.
Phase I of the project will develop a 139-room condominium hotel, and Phase II will add 95 condominium hotel
units and a 108-unit resort hotel. The hotel will include a 16,800 square-foot conference and wellness center, a
14,100 square-foot spa, and three restaurants totaling approximately 19,700 square feet. Impacts of Phase I (139
units) and Project Buildout (342 units total) are analyzed separately.


STUDY AREA
This analysis evaluated the operations of the following ten (10) study intersections, which were selected in
consultation with Sand City, Transportation Agency for Monterey County (TAMC), and Caltrans staff. Intersection
jurisdictions are noted in parentheses.

Intersections

    1. Fremont Boulevard/SR 1/Monterey Road/Ord Avenue (Caltrans jurisdiction)
    2. California Avenue/Playa Avenue (Sand City jurisdiction)
    3. Del Monte Boulevard/Playa Avenue (Seaside jurisdiction)
    4. Fremont Boulevard/Playa Avenue (Seaside jurisdiction)
    5. California Avenue/Tioga Avenue (Sand City jurisdiction)
    6. Del Monte Boulevard/Tioga Avenue (Seaside jurisdiction)
    7. Del Monte Boulevard/Broadway Avenue (Seaside jurisdiction)
    8. SR 218/SR 1 Southbound Ramp (Caltrans jurisdiction)
    9. SR 218/SR 1 Northbound Ramp (Caltrans jurisdiction)
    10. Del Monte Boulevard/Canyon Del Rey Boulevard (Caltrans jurisdiction)

The study also included evaluation of the following freeway and roadway segments (northbound and
southbound). All Freeway segments operate under Caltrans' jurisdiction, while Del Monte Boulevard is under the
City of Seaside's jurisdiction.

Freeway Segments

        SR 1 ­ Del Monte Avenue to SR 218

        SR 1 ­ SR 218 to Sand City/Ord Village (Fremont/California)

ANALYSIS SCENARIOS
Peak traffic conditions generally occur during weekday mornings between 7:00 AM and 9:00 AM and between
4:00 PM and 6:00 PM in the evenings. The operations of the study intersections were evaluated during the
weekday morning (AM) and evening (PM) peak hours for the following six (6) scenarios:

        Scenario 1:       Existing Conditions - Existing volumes obtained from counts, representing peak one-hour
                          traffic conditions during the commute periods.

        Scenario 2:       Existing Plus Project Conditions ­ Scenario 1 volumes plus traffic generated by the
                          Proposed Project. Phase I (139 units) and Project Buildout (342 units total) are analyzed
                          separately.

        Scenario 3:       Background Conditions - Existing peak-hour volumes plus traffic from approved but not
                          yet constructed developments in the study area.

        Scenario 4:       Background Plus Project Conditions ­ Scenario 3 volumes plus traffic generated by the
                          proposed 178-unit resort. Phase I (139 units) and Project Buildout (342 units total) are
                          analyzed separately.

        Scenario 5:       Cumulative Conditions ­ Background conditions traffic volumes (Scenario 3) plus traffic
                          associated with pending development in the area.

        Scenario 6:       Cumulative Plus Project Conditions ­ Scenario 5 volumes plus traffic generated by the
                          proposed project buildout (only full buildout of the 342 units is analyzed under this
                          scenario).

Intersection impacts for the proposed project were evaluated by comparing the intersection operations under the
No Project scenarios (Existing, Background, and Cumulative) to the respective Plus Project scenarios.

LEVEL OF SERVICE METHODOLOGIES
The operations of the intersections and roadway segments were evaluated using Level of Service (LOS)
calculations. Level of Service is a quantitative measure of an intersection's operations, ranging from LOS A, or
free-flow conditions, to LOS F, or over-saturated conditions.

Signalized Intersections

The LOS methodology for signalized intersections described in Chapter 16 of the 2000 Highway Capacity Manual
(HCM) published by the Transportation Research Board was applied in this analysis. This methodology evaluates
a signalized intersection's operations based on average control delay. The new 2010 HCM is available at the time
of this report; however, not many jurisdictions have adopted the 2010 HCM in their analysis, as many LOS
software programs only recently released updated software programs. It should be noted that differences in
analysis results for standard intersection evaluation would be negligible between the 2000 HCM and 2010 and are
unlikely to change the conclusions of this report.

The Synchro analysis software was used for all intersections in this analysis. Table 1 summarizes the relationship
between delay and LOS for signalized intersections. Sand City's minimum acceptable level of service for
signalized intersections is LOS D. The City of Seaside's minimum acceptable level of service for signalized
intersections is LOS C.


                                                     TABLE 1
                               SIGNALIZED INTERSECTION LEVEL OF SERVICE DEFINITIONS
                                    USING AVERAGE CONTROL VEHICULAR DELAY

                                                                                                        Average Control Delay
    Level of                                                                                                 Per Vehicle
    Service                                            Description                                           (Seconds)
                    Operations with very low delay occurring with favorable progression and/or
        A                                                                                                        10
                    short cycle lengths.
                    Operations with low delay occurring with good progression and/or short cycle
        B                                                                                                     10 to 20
                    lengths.
                    Operations with average delays resulting from fair progression and/or longer
        C                                                                                                     20 to 35
                    cycle lengths. Individual cycle failures begin to appear.
                    Operations with longer delays due to a combination of unfavorable progression,
        D           long cycle lengths, and high V/C ratios. Many vehicles stop and individual cycle          35 to 55
                    failures are noticeable.
                    Operations with high delay values indicating poor progression, long cycle
        E                                                                                                     55 to 80
                    lengths, and high V/C ratios. Individual cycle failures are frequent occurrences.
                    Operations with delays unacceptable to most drivers occurring due to over-
        F                                                                                                       > 80
                    saturation, poor progression, or very long cycle lengths.
 Source:    Transportation Research Board, Highway Capacity Manual 2000.



Unsignalized Intersections

Unsignalized intersections (all-way stop controlled and side-street stop controlled) are evaluated using the
Highway Capacity Manual ­ Special Report 209 (Chapter 17) methodologies. Operations are defined by the


                                                                                                                           5
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



average control delay per vehicle (measured in seconds) for each stop-controlled movement. This incorporates
delay associated with deceleration, acceleration, stopping, and moving up in the queue. For side-street stop
controlled intersections, the delay reported in this study is represented for the worst-case minor street approach
and the average intersection delay for the intersection. For all-way stop controlled intersections the level of
service is represented by the average control delay for the whole intersection. Table 2 summarizes the
relationship between delay and LOS for unsignalized intersections. The City of Seaside's minimum acceptable
LOS for all-way stop controlled intersections is LOS C and for two-way stop controlled intersections, the minimum
acceptable LOS for the worst approach is LOS E. The City of Sand City maintains LOS D as the minimum
acceptable LOS for all unsignalized intersections.

                                                        TABLE 2
                               LEVEL OF SERVICE CRITERIA FOR UNSIGNALIZED INTERSECTIONS

    Level of                                                                                               Average Control Delay
    Service                                          Description                                           Per Vehicle (Seconds)
          A                                       Little or no delay.                                                  10
          B                                      Short traffic delays.                                          10 < delay  15
          C                                    Average traffic delays.                                          15 < delay  25
          D                                      Long traffic delays.                                           25 < delay  35
          E                                       Very long delays.                                             35 < delay  50
          F                                    Stop-and-go conditions.                                                > 50
 Source: 2000 Highway Capacity Manual (Transportation Research Board, 2000).



Roadway Segments

Roadway segment evaluations are typically used to establish the number of travel lanes in each direction required
to serve projected daily or peak-hour traffic volumes. For streets in developed areas with numerous cross streets,
intersections govern the operation of the roadway system because the traffic control devices (i.e. traffic signals or
stop signs) control vehicle movements, cause delay, and ultimately establish the vehicle capacity. Arterial
roadway segment levels of service are derived by comparing the hourly volume to the threshold values (based on
roadway type) presented in Table 3.

                                                     TABLE 3
                                                                                      1
                           LEVEL OF SERVICE THRESHOLD VOLUMES FOR UNDIVIDED ARTERIALS

                                                                                                                              2
                                                                           Maximum Hourly Volume (both directions)

 Roadway Type                                                  LOS A            LOS B            LOS C            LOS D            LOS E

 4-Lane Undivided Arterial (with left-turn lanes)               1,600            1,900            2,200            2,400            2,700
 Notes:
   1.         All volumes are approximate and assume ideal roadway characteristics. Actual threshold volumes for each level of service listed
              above may vary depending on a number of factors including curvature and grade, intersection or interchange spacing,
              percentage of trucks and other heavy vehicles, lane widths, signal timing, on-street parking, amount of cross street traffic,
              pedestrians, driveway spacing, etc.
   2.         Non-directional peak hour volumes are normally about 10 percent of the daily volume. Directional split is assumed 60/40.
 Source: City of Seaside General Plan, 2003.

Freeway Segments

Freeway mainline segments were evaluated using the method presented in Caltrans' Guide for the Preparation of
Traffic Impact Studies (December 2002). Caltrans' analysis procedure is based on the density of the traffic flow
using methods described in the 2000 HCM. Density is expressed in vehicles per mile per lane (veh/mi/ln). The
HCS+ software (version 5.3) is used to calculate the freeway segment levels of service. Table 4 presents the
range of densities for freeway mainline segment LOS.


                                                            TABLE 4:
                                                   FREEWAY MAINLINE LOS CRITERIA

                                                                                                                             1
Level of Service                                                   Description                                      Density
            A                  Free-flow speeds prevail. Vehicles are almost completely unimpeded in their             11
                                             ability to maneuver within the traffic stream.
            B               Free-flow speeds are maintained. The ability to maneuver with the traffic stream is     > 11 to 18
                                                        only slightly restricted.
            C                 Flow with speeds at or near free-flow speeds. Freedom to maneuver within the          > 18 to 26
                             traffic stream is noticeably restricted, and lane changes require more care and
                                                     vigilance on the part of the driver.
            D                   Speeds decline slightly with increasing flows. Freedom to maneuver with the         > 26 to 35
                               traffic stream is more noticeably limited, and the driver experiences reduced
                                                    physical and psychological comfort.
            E                Operation at capacity. There are virtually no usable gaps within the traffic stream,   > 35 to 45
                              leaving little room to maneuver. Any disruption can be expected to produce a
                                                         breakdown with queuing.
            F                                           Represents a breakdown in flow.                                 **
        1
Note:       Density in passenger vehicles per mile per lane (veh/mi/ln).
        ** Demand flow exceeds capacity.
Source: Highway Capacity Manual, Transportation Research Board, 2000.


In the Guide for the Preparation of Traffic Impact Studies (2002), Caltrans defines the LOS standards for State-
operated facilities. Caltrans defines the following LOS standards:

            Caltrans endeavors to maintain a target LOS at the transition between LOS C and LOS D

            If an existing State-operated facility is operating at less than LOS C, the existing measure (seconds of
            delay for intersections and density for freeway segments) should be maintained

Signal Warrants

Signal warrant analyses are intended to examine the general correlation between the planned level of future
development and the need to install new traffic signals. It estimates future development-generated traffic
compared against a sub-set of the standard traffic signal warrants recommended in the Federal Highway
Administration's Manual on Uniform Traffic Control Devices (MUTCD) and associated State guidelines. This
analysis should not serve as the only basis for deciding whether and when to install a signal. To reach such a
decision, the full set of warrants should be investigated based on field-measured, rather than forecast, traffic data
and a thorough study of traffic and roadway conditions by an experienced engineer. Furthermore, the decision to
install a signal should not be based solely upon the warrants, since the installation of signals can lead to certain


                                                                                                                                 7
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



types of collisions. The responsible State or local agency should undertake regular monitoring of actual traffic
conditions and accident data, and timely re-evaluation of the full set of warrants in order to prioritize and program
intersections for signalization.


SIGNIFICANT IMPACT CRITERIA
According to the 2010 CEQA Guidelines, implementation of the Proposed Project would have a significant impact
if it would do any of the following:

        Conflict with an applicable plan, ordinance, or policy establishing measures of effectiveness for the
        performance of the circulation system, taking into account all modes of transportation including mass
        transit and non-motorized travel and relevant components of the circulation system, including, but not
        limited to, intersections, streets, highways and freeways, pedestrian and bicycle paths, and mass transit.

        Conflict with an applicable congestion management program, including, but not limited to, level of service
        standards and travel demand measures or other standards established by the county congestion
        management agency for designated roads or highways.

        Result in a change in air traffic patterns, including either an increase in traffic levels or a change in
        location that results in substantial safety risks.

        Substantially increase hazards due to a design feature (e.g., sharp curves or dangerous intersections) or
        incompatible uses (e.g., farm equipment).

        Result in inadequate emergency access.

        Conflict with adopted policies, plans, or programs regarding public transit, bicycle, or pedestrian facilities,
        or otherwise decrease the performance or safety of such facilities.

The CEQA Guidelines are intended to provide general guidance for lead agencies evaluating impacts to the
transportation system. For purposes of evaluating impacts of the Proposed Project, the above significance criteria
are interpreted as described below.

City of Sand City Impact Criteria

The Sand City General Plan specifies that a minimum LOS D is desired for all non-freeway streets and all
intersections within the City. Therefore, a significant impact would occur if the LOS D threshold is exceeded due
to project traffic on a roadway segment or signalized intersection. At unsignalized intersections, a significant
impact would occur if the LOS D threshold is exceeded and the intersection conditions justify the installation of a
traffic signal.

City of Seaside Impact Criteria

The City of Seaside's significance criteria apply to the intersections within the City's boundaries. According to the
Seaside's General Plan, a significant impact at a signalized study intersection in the City of Seaside is defined to
occur under the following scenarios:

        The addition of project traffic causes operations to deteriorate from an acceptable level (LOS C or better)
        to an unacceptable level (LOS D, LOS E, or LOS F), or


                                                                                                                     8
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



        The addition of project traffic increases the average delay more than 2.0 seconds at intersections
        operating at LOS D, or

        The addition of project traffic increases the average delay by more than 1.0 second at intersections
        operating at LOS E or LOS F.

A significant impact at an unsignalized study intersection in the City of Seaside is defined to occur under the
following scenarios:

        The addition of project traffic causes average intersection operations to deteriorate from an acceptable
        level (LOS E or better for two-way stop control, LOS C or better for all-way stop control) to an
        unacceptable level (LOS F for two-way stop control, LOS D for all-way stop control), or

        The addition of project traffic exacerbates the unacceptable operations (LOS F for two-way stop control or
        LOS D for all-way stop control), and

        The MUTCD peak-hour volume signal warrant is met.

California Departmetn of Transportation (Caltrans) Facility Impact Criteria

Caltrans has identified a level of service objective of C/D (i.e. on the "cusp" or threshold between level of service
C and D) as the acceptable service level. However, the Caltrans' Transportation Concept Report for State Route
1 identifies acceptable operations as LOS D for this segment of State Route 1. Additionally the Transportation
Agency for Monterey County (discussed below) has adopted a level of service standard of D for freeway
segments in Monterey County in the Regional Transportation Plan and the Nexus Study for a Regional
Development Impact Fee. For the purpose of this analysis, deficiencies are defined for those facilities operating at
LOS E or worse. Therefore, impacts on mainline segments and signalized intersections are defined to occur when
the addition of project traffic:

        Causes operations to deteriorate from an acceptable level (LOS D) to an unacceptable level (LOS E or
        worse), or

        If an existing State-operated facility is operating at less than LOS D, the existing measure (seconds of
        delay for intersections and density for freeway segments) should be maintained

Impacts at unsignalized intersections are defined to occur when the addition of project traffic:

        Results in unacceptable service levels (LOS E or worse), and

        The MUTCD peak-hour volume signal warrant is met.

The Transportation Agency for Monterey County (TAMC) Mitigation Criteria

TAMC is a 23-member agency, which consists of local officials from each of its incorporated cities and five county
supervisorial districts, as well as ex officio members from six public agencies. The ex officio members include
Caltrans and AMBAG among others. In 2004, TAMC prepared and released the Nexus Study for a Regional
Development Impact Fee. The regional development impact fee program is intended to reduce traffic congestion,
improve the level of service, and mitigate regional and cumulative traffic impacts created by new development.
The fee program sought to come up with a fair share impact fee based on the type and intensity of new
development. In 2006, TAMC updated and revised the development impact fee program. The new program has
been adopted and became effective in August 2008. TAMC has adopted a level of service standard of D for


                                                                                                                   9
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



regional roadways in Monterey County in the Regional Transportation Plan and the Nexus Study for a Regional
Development Impact Fee.

2. EXISTING CONDITIONS
This chapter provides a description of Existing Conditions in terms of roadway facilities, bicycle and pedestrian
facilities, transit service, traffic volumes, intersection operations, and roadway segment operations. The
methodology used to calculate the intersection and roadway segment levels of service and the results are
presented in this chapter.


EXISTING ROADWAY NETWORK
Regional access to the project site is provided by SR 1 and SR 218. Primary local access to the site is provided
by Playa Avenue and Tioga Road. Detailed descriptions of the key roadway facilities are presented below.

SR 1 is a four- to six-lane state freeway located east of the project site. SR 1 extends northward through Santa
Cruz and southward through Monterey and Carmel. North of Fremont Boulevard, SR 1 includes three lanes in
each direction. South of Fremont Boulevard, two lanes in each direction are provided. Access to the project site is
via the SR 1 interchanges at Fremont Boulevard and SR 218.

SR 218 is a four-lane state arterial connecting SR 1 with Highway 68 through Del Rey Oaks. It is known as
Canyon Del Rey Boulevard within the study area. SR 218 is located south of the project site.

Del Monte Boulevard is a four-lane north-south arterial extending from Fremont Avenue through the City of
Seaside, connecting to the City of Monterey.

Fremont Boulevard is a four-lane north-south arterial through the City of Seaside, extending from SR 1 to the City
of Monterey.

California Avenue is a two-lane north-south collector that extends from Contra Costa Street to the Fremont
Boulevard/State SR 1 interchange. Between Tioga Avenue and Playa Avenue, California Avenue passes through
the Sand Dollar shopping center. This segment of California Avenue is not a public street. A future street
connection between Tioga Avenue and Playa Avenue is planned with the existing Transportation Agency for
Monterey County (TAMC) right-of-way

Tioga Avenue is a two-lane east-west collector that runs from the beach to Del Monte Avenue, crossing over SR
1.

Playa Avenue is a two-lane east-west collector that runs from a T-junction with Metz Road to the east beyond
Fremont Boulevard.

Sand Dunes Drive is a two-lane north-south local street that extends from Tioga Avenue to the south past
Humboldt Street. Currently, Sand Dunes Drive terminates at the south end of the project site.


EXISTING BICYCLE FACILITIES
Bikeway planning and design in California typically relies on the guidelines and design standards established by
                                                                                             th
Caltrans in the Highway Design Manual (Chapter 1000: Bikeway Planning and Design, 5 Edition, California
Department of Transportation, January 2001). Chapter 1000 follows standards developed by the American
Association of State Highway and Transportation Officials (AASHTO) and the Federal Highway Administration
(FHWA), and identifies specific design standards for various conditions and bikeway-to-roadway relationships.
Under California Law, bicyclists are allowed to use all roadways in California unless posted closed. Therefore,
even for the roadways that have no designated (or planned) bikeways identified, a majority are open for cycling.


                                                                                                                11
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



Caltrans standards provide for three (3) distinct types of bikeway facilities, as generally described below and
shown on the accompanying figures.

        Class I Bikeway (Bike Path) provides a completely separate right-of-way and is designated for the
        exclusive use of bicycles and pedestrians with vehicle and pedestrian cross-flow minimized. In general,
        bike paths serve corridors not served by streets and highways or where sufficient right-of-way exists to
        allow such facilities to be constructed away from the influence of parallel streets and numerous vehicle
        conflicts.




        Class II Bikeways (Bike Lanes) are lanes for bicyclists adjacent to the outer vehicle travel lanes. These
        lanes have special lane markings, pavement legends, and signage. Bicycle lanes are generally five (5)
        feet wide. Adjacent vehicle parking and vehicle/pedestrian cross-flow are permitted.




        Class III Bikeway (Bike Route) are designated by signs or pavement markings for shared use with
        pedestrians or motor vehicles, but have no separated bike right-of-way or lane striping. Bike routes serve
        either to: a) provide continuity to other bicycle facilities, or b) designate preferred routes through high
        demand corridors.




                                                                                                                12
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



Figure 3 shows the existing bicycle facilities in and near the project site as identified in the City of Seaside's
Bicycle Transportation Plan, 2007. In the study area, a bike path extends from the intersection of Metz
Road/Playa Avenue under SR 1 to the beach, where it continues north to meet with the Monterey Bay Coastal
Trail near the Fremont Boulevard/SR 1/Monterey Road/Ord Avenue intersection. North of this intersection, the
Coastal Trail continues to Castroville. The Coastal Trail is discontinuous between Playa Avenue and Tioga
Avenue, but restarts at Tioga Avenue and parallels Sand Dunes Drive to the southern city border and beyond to
Carmel. Bike lanes are provided on California Avenue, Playa Avenue between California Avenue and Metz Road,
Metz Road, and Tioga Avenue.

EXISTING PEDESTRIAN FACILITIES
Pedestrian facilities include sidewalks, crosswalks, pedestrian signals, and off-street paths that are meant to
provide safe and convenient routes for pedestrians to access destinations such as institutions, businesses, public
transportation, and recreation facilities. Sidewalks and crosswalks are provided at all of the study intersections,
with the exception of the two SR 218/SR 1 Ramps intersections. At these two intersections, the sidewalks are
discontinuous, and there are no crosswalks.

EXISTING TRANSIT SERVICE
Monterey-Salinas Transit (MST) provides fixed-route bus service and shuttle service in Monterey County including
Sand City. The MST Edgewater Transit Exchange is located approximately 0.25 miles from the project site at
Playa Avenue and Metz Road. As shown in Table 5 and illustrated on Figure 4, the Transit Exchange is served
by eight bus routes.

EXISTING TRAFFIC VOLUMES
The operations of the study intersections were evaluated for weekday morning (AM) and evening (PM) peak hour
traffic conditions. Peak traffic conditions generally occur on weekday mornings between 7:00 a.m. and 9:00 a.m.
and evenings between 4:00 p.m. and 6:00 p.m. Intersection operations were evaluated for the one hour during
this period with the highest measured traffic volumes. Peak-hour traffic counts were collected in December 2011
at all of the study intersections. The traffic counts are included in Appendix A. The existing lane configurations
and peak-hour volumes are shown on Figure 5.

3. EXISTING PLUS PROJECT CONDITIONS
The impacts of the proposed development on the surrounding transportation system are discussed in this chapter.
First, the methodology used to estimate the amount of traffic generated by the proposed project is described.
Then, the results of the level of service calculations for Existing Plus Project Conditions are presented. Existing
Plus Project Conditions are defined as Existing Conditions plus traffic generated by the proposed project. Project
impacts are then identified by comparing the level of service results under Project Conditions to those under
Existing Conditions.

The proposed development is divided into two phases. Phase I of the project will develop a 139-room
condominium hotel, and Phase II will add 95 condominium hotel units and a 108-unit resort hotel. The hotel will
include a 16,800 square-foot conference and wellness center, a 14,100-square foot spa, and three restaurants
totaling approximately 19,700 square feet. Impacts of Phase I (139 units) and Project Buildout (342 units total) are
analyzed separately.


PROJECT TRAFFIC ESTIMATES
The amount of automobile traffic associated with the project was estimated using a three-step process: (1) trip
generation, (2) trip distribution, and (3) trip assignment. In the first step, the amount of traffic entering and exiting
the site was estimated on a daily and peak-hour basis. In the second step, the direction vehicles use to approach
and depart the site was estimated. The trips were assigned to specific street segments and intersection turning
movements in the third step. The results of the process for this analysis are described in the following sections.

Trip Generation

Automobile trip generation was calculated using rates from the Institute of Transportation Engineers (ITE) Trip
              th
Generation (8 Edition) and the San Diego Association of Governments' (SANDAG) Traffic Generators (April
2002). While neither of these resources includes a land use category for condominium hotels, they do include a
number of similar uses.

The rates provided for residential condominiums/townhomes, low-rise apartments, and resort hotels were
compared to determine which rate was most appropriate for the proposed condominium hotel. The total trip
generation rates for these uses are similar, but the proportion of trips going in and out are different. The rates for
the residential uses (i.e. the condominium and apartment rates) show most trips leaving in the morning and
returning in the evening, while the hotel rates show most trips arriving in the morning and leaving in the evening.
The high rate of trips inbound during the AM peak and outbound during the PM peak reflects employee trips
to/from the hotel, trips that are not adequately captured by the residential land uses trip rates. The proposed
condominium hotel would cater to vacationers, who typically make more daily trips than residents, but tend to
spread their trips throughout the day. SANDAG's resort hotel land use was selected for use in the analysis
because it is slightly more conservative than ITE's resort hotel rates, and because its rates have been obtained by
surveying similar sites in California.

Trips generated by the conference and wellness center and the spa were assumed to remain internal to the
project site, as most users of these facilities would be hotel or condominium guests. The proposed restaurants will
attract patrons from both within the project site and other external locations. Resort hotels typically include
restaurants, so we assumed that the trip generation for one of the three restaurants is included in the Resort Hotel
trip generation. For the other two restaurants, we assumed that one is a High-Turnover Restaurant (ITE Land Use
Code 932) and that the other is a Quality Restaurant (ITE Land Use Code 931). These two restaurants are
assumed to generate external trips, drawing patrons from locations beyond the project site.


                                                                                                                      20
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



Trip generation estimates for the proposed project are presented below in Table 8. Upon buildout of Phases 1 &
2, the proposed project will generate approximately 3,669 daily trips, 194 morning peak hour trips (112 inbound,
82 outbound) and 279 evening peak hour trips (141 inbound and 138 outbound).

Trip Distribution

Project trip distribution was based on the location of supporting land uses near the project site, and patterns used
in previous traffic studies in the area. Figure 6 shows the proposed trip distribution for the project. Most trips
(65%) are expected to use SR 1 to destinations north and south of the project site. Of these, 40% are expected to
go south to the attractions near Monterey. The remaining trips will be spread along the local and regional surface
street network.

Trip Assignment

Trips generated by the proposed project were assigned to the roadway system based on the directions of
approach and departure described above. Peak hour project trips for Phase I are shown on Figure 9 and peak
hour project trips for the Project Buildout (Phase I & II) are shown in Figure 10. Project-generated trips were
added to existing traffic volumes to estimate volumes under Project Conditions, which are shown on Figure 7 for
Phase I and Figure 8 for Phase I & II combined.

EXISTING PLUS PROJECT INTERSECTION IMPACTS AND MITIGATION MEASURES
This section of the report evaluates the intersection LOS results presented in Table 9 against the City of Sand
City, City of Seaside, and Caltrans' criteria for significant impacts and presents mitigation measures for identified
impacts.

Int. 1. Fremont Boulevard/SR 1/Monterey Road/Ord Avenue

The proposed project will result in a significant traffic impact at the intersection of Fremont Boulevard/SR
1/Monterey Road/Ord Avenue under Existing Plus Phase I and Buildout Project Conditions because it
exacerbates unacceptable LOS F intersection operations during both peak periods.

Caltrans approved the SR 1 Project Study Report (PSR) in 2002, which identified improvements for the
intersection at the Fremont Boulevard/SR 1/Monterey Road/Ord Avenue intersection. Currently, this project is not
fully funded, though the project is included in the Regional Transportation Impact Fee Program that TAMC
adopted in August 2008. The improvements include:

             Eliminating the east leg of the Fremont Boulevard and Monterey Road intersection,

             Prohibiting left-turns from Fremont Boulevard to Monterey Road,

             Realigning Monterey Road to connect with Fremont Boulevard at Military Avenue, and

             Widening SR 1 in the vicinity of the Fremont interchange.

The project volumes were modified to account for the changes in travel patterns from the PSR improvements.
Intersection levels of service with the PSR improvements were calculated and summarized in Table 11. The PSR
improvements would mitigate the intersection impacts at the Fremont Boulevard/SR 1/Monterey Road/Ord
Avenue intersection to less-than-significant levels.

Table 11 summarized the intersection delay and LOS for the mitigation under Phase I and Project Buildout
Conditions.

EXISTING PLUS PROJECT ROADWAY SEGMENT IMPACTS AND MITIGATION MEASURES
The results in Table 10 indicate that the proposed project will result in significant traffic impacts at the following
Caltrans freeway segments under Existing Plus Phase I and Buildout Project Conditions:

        SR 1 from Route 218 to Fremont (Northbound)

        SR 1 from Route 218 to Fremont (Southbound)

        SR 1 from Route 218 to Del Monte (Northbound)

        SR 1 from Route 218 to Del Monte (Southbound)

Implementation of the SR 1 PSR improvements includes widening of SR 1 to six lanes between Fremont
Boulevard and SR 218. Funding for this improvement is not currently in place, though project is included in the
Regional Transportation Impact Fee Program that TAMC adopted in August 2008. Widening the SR 1 segment
between Fremont Boulevard and SR 218 will improve operations to LOS C under both Phase I and Project
Buildout Conditions; thus mitigating the project impacts to less-than-significant levels.

Capacity improvements along SR 1 south of SR 218 would be necessary to mitigate the impacts on the segment
between SR 218 and Del Monte Boulevard. No improvements are currently identified for this segment of SR 1.
Widening SR 1 to six lanes would improve operations at these locations to LOS C or better under both Phase I
and Project Buildout Conditions. Freeway impacts can be mitigated with contribution to the Regional Development
Impact Fee adopted by TAMC.

Transportation Demand Management (TDM) Program

The project proposes a number of TDM measures to reduce the number of vehicle trips and parking demand
generated by the project. Some of the TDM measures include on-demand shuttle service for resort guests,
incorporation of a public transit stop to facilitate employee and visitor use of local public transit, and provision of
designated vanpool parking spaces for employees that carpool to and from work. The analysis presented in this
report does not include any vehicle trip reductions to account for the proposed TDM measures. The successful
implementation of a TDM program will further help to reduce the project's intersection and freeway impacts.


PEDESTRIAN, BICYCLE, AND TRANSIT IMPACTS AND MITIGATION
The City of Sand City does not specify impact criteria for pedestrian, bicycle, and transit impacts. However, these
impacts are generally evaluated based on whether a Proposed Project would: 1) conflict with existing or planned
pedestrian, bicycle, or transit facilities, or 2) create walking, bicycling, or transit use demand without providing
adequate and appropriate facilities for non-motorized mobility.

The proposed project is not expected to increase the biking or transit demand to a level where it could not be
accommodated by existing or planned facilities. Sidewalks are currently provided on the south side of Tioga
Avenue from Del Monte Boulevard to Sand Dunes Drive. To provide pedestrian access from the project site to
Tioga Drive the project should provide a crosswalk across the west and south legs of the Tioga Avenue/Sand
Dunes Drive intersection. While not necessarily required by the City, these improvements are recommended to
encourage biking and walking by resort guests and surrounding users. The Proposed Project would have a less-
than-significant impact on pedestrian, bicycle, and transit facilities and services.




                                                                                                                    30
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



SITE ACCESS AND ON-SITE CIRCULATION
Figure 11 presents the preliminary site plan for the proposed project and indicates suggested improvements and
mitigations to facilitate on-site circulation. For Phase I site access is proposed via one driveway off Tioga Avenue
just west of SR 1. With the construction of Phase II an additional site access point will be provided with the
extension Playa Avenue. Playa Avenue currently terminates east of SR 1 and will be extended through the
existing SR 1 underpass to the project site. The access points are considered adequate to accommodate project
traffic at buildout.

The project roadway that fronts the project site on the east side is known as Sand Dunes Drive. Sand Dunes
Drive provides three access points to the internal project network, as well as access to the public parking lot on
the north end of the project site. For the purpose of this document, the access points from Sand Dunes Drive to
the internal roadway network are labeled as A, B, and C in Figure 11.

Access Point A

Access Point A is located at southern end of the project site. A stop sign should be provided on the side street to
give through traffic on Sand Dunes Drive the right-of-way.

Phase I currently includes the extension of the coastal bike trail from Playa Avenue to Tioga Avenue along the
east side of Sand Dunes Drive. The site plan shows the trail crossing Sand Dunes Drive on the north side of the
Access Point A. To minimize the number of roadway crossings and to increase pedestrian/bicycle visibility it is
recommended that the trail cross Sand Dunes Drive on the south side of Access Point A. Since this would be an
uncontrolled crossing, additional traffic calming measures, such as a raised crosswalk should be installed at the
Sand Dunes Drive crossing.

Additionally, the project proposes a pedestrian crossing on the northwestern leg of Access Point A. Since the
coastal bike trails crossing point should be moved to the south side of this access point, it is recommended that
the pedestrian crossing also be moved to the south side of the intersection. Additionally, sidewalks should be
provided on both sides of Sand Dunes Drive along the project frontage.

Access Point B

A center median, similar to the one provided at Access Point C, should be provided at this intersection with Sand
Dunes Drive. The site plan currently shows a small round median at this access point, which should be extended
along the driveway throat. This will help establish right-of-way and prevent vehicles from cutting across the
intersection.

Similar to Access Point A, this intersection should be side-street stop controlled.

Playa Avenue/Sand Dunes Drive Intersection (Access Point C)

With the construction of Phase II the Playa Avenue/Sand Dunes Drive intersection will serve as a major access
point to the project site because it provides a direct connection to the main Hotel lobby and restaurant area. To
facilitate vehicle flow in and out of the project site, it is recommended that this intersection be all-way stop sign
controlled. Additionally, the east leg of the intersection should be striped for pedestrian crossings and include a
raised crosswalk, since the coastal bike trail crosses Playa Avenue at this location. Providing an all-way stop-
controlled intersection at this location will also facilitate pedestrian and bicycle crossings from the coastal bike
trail.

January 2012
SJ06-848_11
                                                                                                                                                                                  FIGURE 11
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis

Hotel 1 and Hotel 2 Parking

Hotel 1 and Hotel 2 provide parking lots where the traffic flow is reversed from the standard right-hand side traffic
flow. The lots have one access point and provide one-way circulation around the parking area. To minimize
conflict points and driver confusion at the parking lot entrances, it is recommended that the lots be re-striped for
standard counter clockwise traffic flow. This recommendation also requires that the diagonal parking spaces be
realigned to provide access to the new circulation pattern. No parking spaces will be lost with this
recommendation.

Large Cul-De-Sacs

The three large round cul-de-sacs are primarily provided for emergency vehicle access. Driveways access the
cul-de-sacs at varying angles and the large area of pavement can result in driver confusion. Appropriate striping
to direct vehicles exiting and entering the access points around the cul-de-sacs should be provided. An alternative
to striping would be the provision of a circular raised or textured pavement in the middle of the cul-de-sacs. This
too would help define the right-of-way in the cul-de-sacs without reducing access for emergency vehicles.

Emergency Access

The local fire department should also review the final site plan to ensure that adequate emergency vehicle access
is provided. Turning templates should be applied to the final site plan to ensure that all emergency vehicles
accessing the project site can negotiate all required turning movements, including the cul-de-sac at the end of the
project driveways.

Pedestrian Access

Sidewalks are provided throughout the proposed project for pedestrian connectivity. As shown on the preliminary
landscape plan, there are sufficient sidewalks and crossing provided. There are no dead end sidewalks, and
where possible pathways are provided through the development to facilitate walkability of the project site.
Crosswalks are provided at roadway intersections where sidewalks are provided. Once more detailed plans are
available, they should be reviewed to ensure ADA compliance.

Signing and Striping

In addition to the location of stop signs indicated above, it is recommend that at minimum stop signs be provided
at the following location noted on Figure 11.

Ultimately, the project applicant should provide a final signing and striping plan for review before project
completion.


PARKING
As proposed, the project would provide 697 parking spaces spread throughout the project site in surface and
underground lots. Chapter 18.64 of Sand City's Municipal Code provides parking requirements for a variety of
land uses. The code requires one space for each hotel room and one space per 50 square feet of restaurant floor
area. The code does not specify a parking requirement for a spa, but it does require one space per 300 square
feet of visitor serving commercial uses, which is the land use most similar to a spa. Since the project is in the
coastal zone, the project must provide an additional 10% of the project's total required parking for public parking,
which may be located on-site or at another location that would benefit public access.

Like the trip generation estimates provided in Table 8, the parking requirements for the project restaurants are
calculated based on the assumption that parking for one of the proposed restaurants is included in the hotel's


                                                                                                                  33
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



parking rate. Most diners at the hotel restaurant would walk to the restaurant from their room. Similarly, the
parking required for the spa has been reduced by 50% because most of the spa's patrons are expected to be
hotel guests. Table 12 summarizes the project's required parking.

As shown in Table 12, the City's code requires that the project provide 692 spaces, which means the site will
have five more parking spaces than required based on the City's code. However, 73 of the 697 spaces shown on
the site plan are tandem spaces. It appears that the 697 spaces is obtained by counting each tandem space as
two single spaces at some locations on the project site and one single space at other locations.

The majority of the tandem parking spaces (51) are located in building C-2, on the south edge of the project near
Tioga Avenue. This building has two levels of parking and three levels of condo hotel units. Since the parking
calculations in Table 12 assume one parking space per condo hotel unit, it would be necessary for multiple units
to share a single tandem parking space if the project were to receive a two-space credit for each tandem space. It
would be infeasible for this to occur without valet parking, which seems unlikely for this type of unit. Therefore,
these tandem spaces should be counted as a single space since each will serve the demand of only one condo
hotel unit.


                                                          TABLE 12
                                                PROJECT PARKING REQUIREMENTS

              Use                                 Size                         Spaces Required          Spaces Provided
      Hotel & Condo Hotel                      342 rooms                               342                    n/a
                     1
               Spa                        14,100 square feet                            24                    n/a
                         2
          Restaurants                     13,133 square feet                           263                    n/a
                                                              Subtotal                 629                    n/a
                               Additional Public Parking Required                       63                    n/a
                                                                 Total                 692                   697
 1.       Assumes that 50% of the spa patrons will walk to the spa from other locations on site.
 2.       Assumes parking for 1/3 of the restaurant space is accounted for in the hotel parking rate.
Source: City of Sand City Municipal Code, Fehr & Peers, January 2012


The parking supply as required by the City's code assumes average attendance at the project sites' facilities. In
cases where attendance at the banquet and conference facilities is higher due to special events such as
conferences or weddings the site should be adequate to meet the parking demand assuming that a high number
of attendees are also guest of the hotel facilities. Alternatively, in cases where a high number of non-hotel guests
are anticipated at high attendance special events at the banquet and conference facilities, the applicant should
prepare a parking management plan that provides, at a minimum, the following information in order to ensure
adequate parking for the project:

         Proposed valet operations, including the location of spaces that will be reserved for valet use.

         Proposed operating plan for large events, such as conferences or weddings. If such events are
         anticipated, it would be necessary to provide a shuttle from a remote parking lot to the project site to
         ensure that the public parking on Tioga Avenue and the Granite parcel remains available for general
         public use.

         Location of spaces reserved for a specific use, such as the spa, restaurants or hotel guests.



                                                                                                                          34
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



        A revised circulation plan incorporating the comments in the Site Access and Circulation section above.

        Proposed additional spaces on the resort site that could be reserved to meet the public parking
        requirements of the project if planned public spaces are lost due to erosion.

Collection at Monterey Bay

April 2012



4. BACKGROUND CONDITIONS
This chapter discusses intersection operations under Background Conditions without and with the proposed
project. Background Conditions are comprised of existing traffic volumes from counts plus estimated traffic
generated by approved developments in the area. This chapter describes the procedure used to determine
background traffic volumes and the results of the level of service analysis for Background Conditions. Impacts are
then identified by comparing the level of service results under Background No Project Conditions to those under
Background Plus Project Conditions. Phase I (139 units) and Project Buildout (342 units total) are analyzed
separately.


BACKGROUND NO PROJECT TRAFFIC ESTIMATES
The traffic volumes for Background No Project Conditions were estimated by adding existing volumes to traffic
generated by approved but not yet constructed projects in the vicinity of the site. A list of approved, but not yet
constructed projects was obtained from the City of Sand City, City of Seaside, City of Marina, City of Monterey,
City of Del Rey Oaks, and Monterey County. Appendix E summarizes these developments and the
corresponding trip generation estimates for each. The resulting background traffic volumes are presented on
Figure 12.


BACKGROUND NO PROJECT ROADWAY IMPROVEMENTS
No study area roadway improvements were assumed to occur because of the above approved projects under this
scenario.


BACKGROUND INTERSECTION LEVELS OF SERVICE
Intersection operations were evaluated with level of service calculations and the results are summarized in Table
13. The intersection LOS calculation sheets for Background No Project and Background Plus Project Conditions
are included in Appendix B.

Signalized Intersections

Under Background Plus Project Conditions, all signalized study intersections are projected to operate at
acceptable service levels during both peak periods with the exception of:

    Int. # 1.    Fremont Boulevard/SR 1/Monterey Road/Ord Avenue will continue to operate at an unacceptable
                 LOS F during the AM and PM peak hours. (Phase I and Project Buildout)

Unsignalized Intersections

Under Background Plus Project Conditions, all unsignalized study intersections are projected to operate at
acceptable service levels during both peak periods with the exception of:

    Int. # 9.    SR 218/SR 1 Northbound Ramps will operate at an unacceptable LOS E under Project Buildout
                 during the AM peak hour

The intersection satisfies the peak-our volume signal warrant during the AM peak hour. Appendix C contains the
peak-hour signal warrants. The peak-hour signal warrant analysis should not serve as the only basis for deciding


                                                                                                                36
Collection at Monterey Bay
Draft Transportation Impact Analysis
April 2012



whether and when to install a traffic signal. The responsible state or local agency should undertake regular
monitoring of actual traffic conditions and accident data and timely re-evaluation of the full set of warrants in order
to prioritize and program intersections for signalization.
